⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الامام أبو بكر بن فورك أن اباه هو أبو بشر اسماعيل بن اسحق، وأنه كان سنيا جماعيا حديثيا أوصى عند وفاته إلى زكريا بن الساجي رحمه رحمه الله وهو إمام فى الفقه والحديث وله كتب منها كتاب اختلاف الفقهاء وكان يذهب مذهب الشافعي وقد روى عنه الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعرى فى كتاب التفسير أحاديث كثيرة يعني الساجي، قلت والصحيح أن أبا بشر جده اسحق كما سبق. وفى نسبة أصحابه أباه إلى ابي بشر تكذيب لأبي على الأهوازى فيما اختلق فإنه زعم انه غير صحيح النسب وانه ما كنى عن اسم أبيه إلا لهذا السبب ولو كانت له بأسماء الرحال وأنسابهم عناية لفرق بين قولنا كنية وكناية وفي إطباق الناس على تسميته بالأشعرى تكذيب لما قاله هذا المفترى وقد ورد عن الرسول المنتخب فيمن يطعن بغير علم في النسب ما أخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم زاهر ابن طاهر الشحامي أنا أبو بكر أحمد بن الحسين بن على البيهقى انا أبو بكر ابن فورك انا عبد الله بن جعفر نا یونس بن حبیب نا أبو داود نا شعبة عن علقمة والمسعودى عن بن مرثد الحضرمي عن أبي الربيع عن أبى هريرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال ( أربع من أمر الجاهلية لن يدعهن الناس : الطعن في الأنساب والنياحة على الميت والأنواء والأعداء أجرب فأجرب مائة فمن أجرب البعير الأول )
And the Imam Abu Bakr ibn Furak mentioned that his father was Abu Bishr Ismail ibn Ishaq, and that he was a Sunni, a follower of the community, a hadith scholar. He appointed upon his death Zakariyya ibn al-Saji (may Allah have mercy on him) as executor, and he was an imam in fiqh and hadith. He authored books including the Book on Differences of the Jurists and he followed the school of al-Shafi'i. And the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari narrated many hadiths from him in the Book of Tafsir, meaning al-Saji. I say: The correct is that Abu Bishr is his grandfather Ishaq, as previously. And the attribution of his father by his companions to Abu Bishr is a refutation of Abu Ali al-Ahwazi regarding what he fabricated, for he claimed that his lineage was not authentic and that he was only given a kunya from his father for this reason. If he had knowledge of the names of men and their lineages, he would distinguish between our saying 'kunya' and 'kinaya'. And the consensus of people in calling him al-Ash'ari is a refutation of what this slanderer said. And there has come from the Chosen Messenger, regarding one who attacks lineage without knowledge, what we were informed by the Shaykh Abu al-Qasim Zahir ibn Tahir al-Shihami: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn al-Husayn ibn Ali al-Bayhaqi informed us: Abu Bakr ibn Furak informed us: Abd Allah ibn Ja'far told us: Yunus ibn Habib told us: Abu Dawud told us: Shu'ba from 'Alqama and al-Mas'udi from Ibn Marthad al-Hadrami from Abu al-Rabi' from Abu Hurayra from the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, who said: 'Four things from the pre-Islamic period of ignorance will not be abandoned by people: attacking lineages, wailing over the dead, (belief in) rain stars, and the sick camel. So if a camel gets mange, a hundred others get mange? Whoever causes the first camel to get mange.'
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage combines Ibn Furak's biographical remark about al-Ash'ari's father, the author's correction, and a refutation of a false claim about the lineage, supported by a hadith. First, Ibn Furak is reported to have said that al-Ash'ari's father was Abu Bishr Ismail, a Sunni hadith scholar who followed the Shafi'i school and left his estate to Zakariyya al-Saji, from whom al-Ash'ari narrated. The author, Ibn Asakir, then corrects this: the correct genealogy is that Abu Bishr is actually al-Ash'ari's grandfather Ishaq. Next, he refutes the accusation of Abu Ali al-Ahwazi, who claimed the lineage was unsound and that the lack of a clear father's name indicated a problem. Ibn Asakir argues that the widespread use of the nisba 'al-Ash'ari' by all people disproves this slander, and that al-Ahwazi confuses 'kunya' (honorific) with 'kinaya' (allusion). Finally, he cites a hadith from the Prophet condemning the practice of attacking lineages without knowledge, thereby underscoring the gravity of such false allegations.

Key Terms
زكريا بن الساجي
أبو على الأهوازى
الطعن في الأنساب
الكنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's lineage is unsound because his father's name is not clearly known or was hidden.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that the claim is based on confusion: Abu Bishr is actually his grandfather. The widespread use of 'al-Ash'ari' and the companions' attribution refute the slander. The hadith warns against attacking lineages without knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Asakir identify as the correct relationship between Abu Bishr and al-Ash'ari?
A) Abu Bishr is his brother
B) Abu Bishr is his father
C) Abu Bishr is his grandfather
D) Abu Bishr is his uncle

Key Takeaway
Correcting lineage errors and refuting slander requires both scholarly evidence and prophetic guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken about someone's family background without verifying the truth? How does the hadith in this lesson make you feel about the weight of your words on that person's honor? Will you seek forgiveness and resolve to never repeat such slander?
?
This lesson shows a scholar publicly correcting a mistake about his own teacher's lineage to defend the truth. Is there an area in your life where you have clung to a falsehood about yourself or others out of pride? Are you willing to humble yourself and correct it as Ibn Asakir did?
?
The slanderer's intent was to damage al-Ash'ari's reputation. When you hear gossip or negative remarks about another Muslim, what is your immediate response based on this lesson? Will you commit to speaking only what you know to be true and defending the honor of others, especially scholars?

The Lineage of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari
Two chains trace the genealogy of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari to Qahtan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two chains of transmission that trace the genealogy of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari back to Qahtan, and the significance of this lineage for the Ash'ari family.

Core Idea
The noble Arab lineage of al-Ash'ari is established through multiple reliable chains.

This passage from Ibn 'Asakir's Tabyin Kidhb al-Muftari presents two detailed chains of transmission that trace the genealogy of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, a companion of the Prophet and the ancestor of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The chains confirm the Arab origin of the Ash'ari family back to Qahtan, a foundational figure in Arab genealogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two chains
The passage begins with a chain from al-Samarqandi via Abu Ubayd giving a concise lineage, then a second chain via al-Anmati and Hisham ibn al-Kalbi providing a more extended genealogy. Notice the different narrators.
Both chains are carefully cited to establish authenticity.
2
Examine the genealogical details
The first chain lists a few key names: Abu Musa ibn Qays from the offspring of al-Jamahir ibn al-Ash'ar. The second chain traces from Qahtan through many generations down to al-Ash'ar and then to Abu Musa. Compare the two versions.
The second chain includes the full line from Qahtan to Abu Musa, while the first is a summary.
3
Note the mention of Abu Musa's mother
The passage also provides the name of Abu Musa's mother, Zabyah bint Wahb, and notes her acceptance of Islam and death in Medina. This adds biographical detail.
This detail is unique to the first chain.
4
Understand the significance
This detailed genealogy establishes the Arabness and noble lineage of al-Ash'ari, important for his credibility and the prestige of the Ash'ari school. It also demonstrates the rigor of early Islamic biographical literature.
The use of multiple chains is a hallmark of hadith methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما نسب جده أبي موسى الأشعرى رضى الله عنه فأخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم اسماعيل بن أحمد بن السمرقندي انا أبو الحسين أحمد بن ابن أحمد بن النقور البزاز انا أبو القسم عيسى بن علی بن عیسی أنا عبد الله بن محمد بن عبد العزيز قال حدثني عمی یعنی علی بن عبد العزيز عن أبي عبيد قال أبو موسى عبد الله بن قيس من ولد الجماهر بن الأشعر (۱) بن أدد قال عبدالله وقال غیر ابی عبید عبدالله بن قيس بن سليم ابن حضار بن حرب بن عامر بن عتر بن بكر بن عامر بن عذر بن وائل بن ناجية بن الجماهر بن الأشعر وهو نبت بن ادد بن يشجب بن عريب بن زید ابن كهلان بن سبأ بن يشجب بن يعرب بن قحطان . قحطان . وأم أبى وأم أبي موسى ظبية بنت وهب بن عك كانت أسلمت وماتت بالمدينة. وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو البركات عبد الوهاب بن المبارك ابن أحمد الأنماطى الحافظ ببغداد قال انا أبو طاهر أحمد بن الحسن بن أحمد وأبو الفضل أحمد بن الحسن بن خيرون الباقلانيان، وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو العز ثابت بن منصور بن المبارك الكيلي ببغداد أنا أبو طاهر أحمد بن الحسن قالا أنا أبو الحسين محمد بن الحسن بن أحمد بن محمد بن موسى بن عمران الأصبهاني أنا أبو الحسين محمد بن أحمد بن اسحق انا أبو حفص عمر بن أحمد بن أسحق الأهوازي نا شباب خليفة بن خياط العصفری نا هشام بن الكلبي عن أبيه قال يقولون ولد قحطان المرعف وهو يعرب فولد يعرب يشجب فولد يشجب سبا وهو عامر فولد سبا كهلان فولد كهلان زيدا فولد زيد عريبا فولد عريب يشجب فولد يشجب بن عريب زيدا فولد زيد ادد بن زيد فولد ادد بن زيد نبتا، وهو الأشعر قال شباب فمن الأشعريين أبو موسى الأشعرى عبد الله بن قيس بن سليم بن حضار بن حرب بن عامر بن عتر بن بكر بن عامر بن عذر ابن وائل بن ناجية بن جماهر بن الأشعر بن ادد بن
As for the lineage of his grandfather Abu Musa al-Ash'ari (may Allah be pleased with him), we were informed by the Shaykh Abu al-Qasim Ismail ibn Ahmad ibn al-Samarqandi: Abu al-Husayn Ahmad ibn ibn Ahmad ibn al-Naqqur al-Bazzaz informed us: Abu al-Qasim Isa ibn Ali ibn Isa informed us: Abd Allah ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Aziz told us, he said: My uncle, meaning Ali ibn Abd al-Aziz, told me from Abu Ubayd, he said: Abu Musa is Abd Allah ibn Qays, from the offspring of al-Jamahir ibn al-Ash'ar (1) ibn Adad. Abd Allah said: And other than Abu Ubayd said: Abd Allah ibn Qays ibn Sulaym ibn Hadar ibn Harb ibn Amir ibn Itr ibn Bakr ibn Amir ibn Udhr ibn Wail ibn Najiya ibn al-Jamahir ibn al-Ash'ar, and he is Nabit ibn Adad ibn Yashjub ibn Yarib ibn Zayd ibn Kahlan ibn Saba ibn Yashjub ibn Yarib ibn Qahtan. Qahtan. And the mother of his father and the mother of Abu Musa was Zabyah bint Wahb ibn Akk, she accepted Islam and died in Medina. And we were informed by the Shaykh Abu al-Barakat Abd al-Wahhab ibn al-Mubarak ibn Ahmad al-Anmati the memorizer in Baghdad, he said: Abu Tahir Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Ahmad and Abu al-Fadl Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Khayrun al-Baqilaniyan informed us, and we were informed by the Shaykh Abu al-Izz Thabit ibn Mansur ibn al-Mubarak al-Kili in Baghdad: Abu Tahir Ahmad ibn al-Hasan told us, they both said: Abu al-Husayn Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Musa ibn Imran al-Isbahani informed us: Abu al-Husayn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Ishaq informed us: Abu Hafs Umar ibn Ahmad ibn Ishaq al-Ahwazi told us: Shabab Khalifa ibn Khayyat al-Usfuri told us: Hisham ibn al-Kalbi told us from his father, he said: They say: Qahtan gave birth to al-Mar'af, who is Yarib. Then Yarib gave birth to Yashjub. Then Yashjub gave birth to Saba, who is 'Amir. Then Saba gave birth to Kahlan. Then Kahlan gave birth to Zayd. Then Zayd gave birth to 'Urayb. Then 'Urayb gave birth to Yashjub. Then Yashjub ibn 'Urayb gave birth to Zayd. Then Zayd gave birth to Adad ibn Zayd. Then Adad ibn Zayd gave birth to Nabit, who is al-Ash'ar. Shabab said: From the Ash'aris is Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Abd Allah ibn Qays ibn Sulaym ibn Hadar ibn Harb ibn Amir ibn Itr ibn Bakr ibn Amir ibn Udhr ibn Wail ibn Najiya ibn Jamahir ibn al-Ash'ar ibn Adad ibn
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed genealogical account of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, the companion and ancestor of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. It begins with a chain from al-Samarqandi via Abu Ubayd, giving a concise lineage: Abu Musa ibn Qays from the offspring of al-Jamahir ibn al-Ash'ar ibn Adad. A second, more detailed version is then given by another chain, tracing from Qahtan down to Nabit, who is al-Ash'ar, and then to Abu Musa. The lineage includes multiple generations: Abu Musa ibn Qays ibn Sulaym ibn Hadar ibn Harb ibn Amir ibn Itr ibn Bakr ibn Amir ibn Udhr ibn Wail ibn Najiya ibn Jamahir ibn al-Ash'ar (Nabit) ibn Adad ibn... (the text ends here due to the page break). The passage also mentions the name of Abu Musa's mother and notes her conversion to Islam and death in Medina. A second chain via al-Anmati and others through Hisham ibn al-Kalbi provides an alternative route, tracing the ancestry from Qahtan (Yarib) through Saba, Kahlan, and then to al-Ash'ar, confirming the same line. This extensive documentation establishes the noble Arab lineage of al-Ash'ari through a renowned companion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lineage of al-Ash'ari is uncertain or lacks reliable transmission.
Correction: Ibn 'Asakir provides two independent chains of transmission, each tracing back to authoritative genealogists, firmly establishing the lineage.

Key Takeaway
The lineage of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari is authenticated through two chains of transmission, confirming his descent from Qahtan and establishing the noble Arab origin of the Ash'ari family.

The Lineage of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari
Ibn Asakir narrates the lineage of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari through multiple isnads.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the genealogical lineage of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari as recorded by Ibn Asakir, including his descent from the Ash'ari tribe and the Qahtani line.

Core Idea
Abu Musa al-Ash'ari's lineage traces back to Qahtan through the Ash'ari tribe.

In this lesson, we examine Ibn Asakir's detailed genealogical report on Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. The passage presents two chains of transmission that establish his descent from the tribe of al-Ash'ar, connecting him to the larger Qahtani lineage. This genealogy is important for understanding the tribal identity of the early Muslim community and the background of a prominent companion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the two chains of transmission. Pay attention to the names and how they connect.
The first chain goes through Abu Ubayd, the second through Hisham ibn al-Kalbi.
2
Identify the two genealogical accounts
Distinguish between the two versions. The first gives a shorter lineage, the second provides a more detailed chain from Qahtan down to al-Ash'ar.
The text explicitly says 'وقال غیر ابی عبید' indicating a second source.
3
Trace the lineage to al-Ash'ar
Follow the second chain to see how 'al-Ash'ar' (Nabt) is derived from Qahtan through a series of ancestors. Note that al-Ash'ar means 'the hairy one' and is a nickname for Nabt ibn Udad.
This lineage is also mentioned in other sources.
4
Note the historical information
Read the concluding sentences about Abu Musa's governorship of Basra and Kufa and his conquests. This adds context to his role in early Islamic history.
The passage shows his significance as a companion and governor.

As for the lineage of his grandfather Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, may God be pleased with him: Sheikh Abu al-Qasim Isma'il ibn Ahmad ibn al-Samarqandi informed us, Abu al-Husayn Ahmad ibn Ibn Ahmad ibn al-Naqqur al-Bazzaz told us, Abu al-Qasim Isa ibn Ali ibn Isa told us, Abdullah ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Aziz told us, he said: my uncle - meaning Ali ibn Abd al-Aziz - narrated to me from Abu Ubayd, who said: Abu Musa is Abdullah ibn Qays from the descendants of al-Jamahir ibn al-Ash'ar ibn Udad. Abdullah said: and other than Abu Ubayd said: Abdullah ibn Qays ibn Sulaym ibn Haddar ibn Harb ibn Amir ibn Itr ibn Bakr ibn Amir ibn Udhra ibn Wail ibn Naji(y)a ibn al-Jamahir ibn al-Ash'ar, and he is Nabt ibn Udad ibn Yashjub ibn Urayb ibn Zayd ibn Kahlan ibn Saba ibn Yashjub ibn Ya'rib ibn Qahtan. And the mother of his father and the mother of Abu Musa was Zabyah bint Wahb ibn 'Akk; she embraced Islam and died in Medina. And Sheikh Abu al-Barakat Abd al-Wahhab ibn al-Mubarak ibn Ahmad al-Anmati al-Hafiz in Baghdad informed us, he said: Abu Tahir Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Ahmad and Abu al-Fadl Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Khayrun al-Baqilaniyan told us, and Sheikh Abu al-Izz Thabit ibn Mansur ibn al-Mubarak al-Kili in Baghdad informed us, Abu Tahir Ahmad ibn al-Hasan said: they both said: Abu al-Husayn Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Musa ibn Imran al-Asbahani told us, Abu al-Husayn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Ishaq told us, Abu Hafs Umar ibn Ahmad ibn Ishaq al-Ahwazi narrated to us, Shabab Khalifa ibn Khayyat al-Usfuri narrated to us, Hisham ibn al-Kalbi narrated to us from his father, who said: They say: Qahtan begat al-Mar'if, who is Ya'rib, and Ya'rib begat Yashjub, and Yashjub begat Saba, who is 'Amir, and Saba begat Kahlan, and Kahlan begat Zayd, and Zayd begat 'Urayb, and 'Urayb begat Yashjub, and Yashjub ibn 'Urayb begat Zayd, and Zayd begat Udad ibn Zayd, and Udad ibn Zayd begat Nabt, and he is al-Ash'ar. Shabab said: Among the Ash'aris is Abu Musa al-Ash'ari Abdullah ibn Qays ibn Sulaym ibn Haddar ibn Harb ibn Amir ibn Itr ibn Bakr ibn Amir ibn Udhra ibn Wail ibn Naji(y)a ibn Jamahir ibn al-Ash'ar ibn Udad ibn Zayd. He was governor of Basra for Umar and Uthman, may God be pleased with them, and he had many conquests there; and he governed Kufa, and there he had a house and children at the site of the Jami' Mosque.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a genealogical report. Ibn Asakir provides two chains of transmission to establish the lineage of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. The first chain goes through Abu Ubayd and others, stating that Abu Musa's full name is Abdullah ibn Qays, descended from al-Jamahir ibn al-Ash'ar ibn Udad. It also mentions his mother's lineage. The second, longer chain goes through Hisham ibn al-Kalbi and details the entire line from Qahtan down to al-Ash'ar (Nabt), then lists the precise ancestry of Abu Musa. The passage concludes with historical notes about Abu Musa's governorship of Basra and Kufa, his conquests, and his family's presence in Kufa. The chains serve to authenticate the genealogy and connect the Ash'ari tribe to the larger Qahtani lineage.

Key Terms
نسب
أبو موسى الأشعري
الأشعر
قحطان
الجماهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The genealogy of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari is meant to prove his religious superiority over other companions.
Correction: The genealogy is a historical record establishing his tribal lineage; it does not imply any inherent religious superiority.

Checkpoint
Who is identified as al-Ash'ar (the hairy one) in the genealogy of Abu Musa?
A) Ya'rib
B) Nabt
C) Udad
D) Jamahir

Key Takeaway
Abu Musa al-Ash'ari traced his ancestry back to Qahtan through the tribe of al-Ash'ar, and his lineage is recorded with multiple chains to ensure authenticity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a detailed record of Abu Musa's lineage, preserved through multiple chains. How carefully have you preserved the Islamic knowledge you received from your teachers? Will you take a concrete step today to document or transmit one thing you have learned, so that it is not lost?
?
Abu Musa's mother Zabyah embraced Islam and died in Medina. Her faith is recorded here, though she is not a famous figure. Do you honor the quiet sacrifices of the women in your own life who have supported your faith? What will you do this week to express gratitude to them?
?
The passage traces ancestry back to Qahtan, emphasizing roots over worldly achievements. In your daily life, do you measure your worth more by your lineage in faith (taqwa) or by your status and accomplishments? How can you shift your focus to what truly matters in Allah's sight?

The Lineage of Qahtan: Conflicting Reports
Two reports on Qahtan's lineage from Isma'il or Sam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main genealogical claims about Qahtan and why such disagreements do not affect Islamic creed.

Core Idea
Genealogical debates among scholars do not undermine religious truth.

Ibn Asakir presents two conflicting accounts of Qahtan's lineage: one tracing him to Isma'il, the other to Sam. This lesson examines these reports and their transmission chains, highlighting that such disagreements are matters of historical investigation, not theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the two views presented.
2
Analyze the chains of narration
Identify the two main reports: one from Ibn Abbas via Hisham al-Kalbi, and another from Muhammad ibn Sa'd. Notice the correction of 'thirty fathers' to 'three'.
The author points out a mistake in the first report.
3
Reflect on the nature of the disagreement
Consider that this is a genealogical dispute among scholars, not a doctrinal issue. Ask yourself why Ibn Asakir includes both views.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال شباب ونا هشام بن محمد بن السائب الكلبى قال حدثني ابي عن ابي صالح عن ابن عباس قال قحطان من ولد اسماعيل بن ابراهيم وبينه وبين اسماعيل ثلاثون أبا، قال وقال أبى لم يزل قحطان يعرفون ذلك وينتسبون اليه حتى كان زمن الحجاج، كذا قال والصواب ثلاثة آباء اخبرنا الشيخ أبو بكر محمد بن عبد الباقي بن محمد الانصاري ببغداد انا أبو محمد الحسن بن علی بن محمد الجوهرى انا أبو عمر محمد بن العباس بن حيوية الخزاز نا أبو الحسن بن معروف ابن بشر الخشاب نا الحسين بن محمد بن عبدالرحمن بن الفهم الفقيه نا محمد بن سعد كاتب الواقدى قال إلى قحطان جماع اليمن فمن نسبه إلى اسماعيل بن ابراهيم قال قحطان بن الهميسع بن تيم بن نبت بن اسماعیل ابن ابراهيم صلى الله عليهما، هكذا كان ينسبه هشام بن السائب الكلبي عن ابيه ويذكر عن أبيه أنه أدرك اهل النسب والعلم ينسبون قحطان إلى اسماعيل بن ابراهيم (۱) ومن نسبه إلى غير ذلك قال قحطان ابن فالغ بن عابر بن ارفخشد بن سام بن نوح صلى الله عليه وسلم، وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو القاسم بن السمرقندى أنا أبو الحسين بن النقور انا محمد بن عبدالرحمن المخلص انا رضوان بن أحمد الصيدلاني نا أحمد ابن عبد الجبار العطاردی نا یونس بن بكير عن ابن اسحق قال ابراهیم بن آذر وهو في التوراة تارخ بن ناحور بن ارغو بن سارخ بن فالغ بن عابر بن شالخ هذا ارفخشذ بن سام بن نوح بن لمك بن متوشلخ بن خنوخ بن برد بن مهلاييل بن قمعان بن انوش بن شيث بن آدم ابى البشر عليه السلام وقال غیره قينان وقد اختلف في نسب ابراهيم عليه أفضل السلام وقول ابن اسحق مكتفى به عن قول غيره من علماء الإسلام.
Shabab said: And Hisham ibn Muhammad ibn al-Sa'ib al-Kalbi narrated to us, he said: my father narrated to me from Abu Salih from Ibn Abbas, he said: Qahtan is from the offspring of Isma'il ibn Ibrahim, and between him and Isma'il are thirty fathers. He said: And my father said: Qahtan's people continued to know that and trace their lineage to him until the time of al-Hajjaj. This is how he said, but the correct (view) is three fathers. Sheikh Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Abd al-Baqi ibn Muhammad al-Ansari in Baghdad informed us, Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Muhammad al-Jawhari told us, Abu Umar Muhammad ibn al-Abbas ibn Hayyawayh al-Khazzaz told us, Abu al-Hasan ibn Ma'ruf ibn Bishr al-Khashshab told us, al-Husayn ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Fahm al-Faqih told us, Muhammad ibn Sa'd, secretary of al-Waqidi, told us: To Qahtan is the gathering of Yemen; so one who traces his lineage to Isma'il ibn Ibrahim said: Qahtan is ibn al-Humaysa' ibn Taym ibn Nabt ibn Isma'il ibn Ibrahim, peace be upon them both. Thus Hisham ibn al-Sa'ib al-Kalbi used to trace his lineage from his father, and he mentions from his father that he met experts in lineage and knowledge who traced Qahtan to Isma'il ibn Ibrahim. And one who traces his lineage otherwise said: Qahtan is ibn Faligh ibn 'Abir ibn Arfakhshad ibn Sam ibn Nuh, peace be upon him. And Sheikh Abu al-Qasim ibn al-Samarqandi informed us, Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Naqqur told us, Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Mukhlis told us, Ridwan ibn Ahmad al-Saydalani told us, Ahmad ibn Abd al-Jabbar al-Utaridi told us, Yunus ibn Bukayr told us from Ibn Ishaq, he said: Ibrahim is ibn Azar, and in the Torah: Tarih ibn Nahur ibn Arghu ibn Sarigh ibn Faligh ibn 'Abir ibn Shalikh, this Arfakhshad ibn Sam ibn Nuh ibn Lamak ibn Mattushalakh ibn Akhnukh ibn Bard ibn Mahla'il ibn Qaynan ibn Anush ibn Shith ibn Adam, father of mankind, peace be upon him. And others said: Qaynan. And there is disagreement concerning the lineage of Ibrahim, peace be upon him, and the statement of Ibn Ishaq suffices from the statement of others among the scholars of Islam.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage discusses the contested genealogy of Qahtan, the eponymous ancestor of the Yemeni tribes. Two main views are presented through separate chains. The first, from Ibn Abbas via Hisham al-Kalbi, asserts that Qahtan is a descendant of Isma'il ibn Ibrahim, with thirty ancestors between them (though later corrected to three). The second view, from Muhammad ibn Sa'd, offers two possibilities: either Qahtan descends from Isma'il as above, or from Faligh ibn 'Abir, linking to Sam ibn Nuh. The passage also includes a full lineage of Ibrahim from Ibn Ishaq, aligning with the Torah. Ibn Asakir concludes that Ibn Ishaq's account is sufficient, emphasizing that scholarly consensus is not needed to accept a sound narration.

Key Terms
قحطان
اسماعيل
هشام الكلبي
ابن عباس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That genealogical disagreements undermine Islamic creed or authority.
Correction: Such disagreements are matters of historical investigation and do not affect the fundamentals of faith. Scholars can hold different views on genealogical details.

Checkpoint
According to the report from Ibn Abbas, how many fathers are between Qahtan and Isma'il?
A) Thirty
B) Three
C) Ten
D) Fifty

Key Takeaway
Genealogical disagreements among reliable scholars do not compromise Islamic teachings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of a scholarly disagreement on a minor matter like Qahtan's lineage, do you ever use it as an excuse to doubt the reliability of Islamic scholarship as a whole? How can you respond next time that thought arises?
?
Ibn Asakir transmitted both reports despite one being corrected. Are you willing to generously engage with views you believe are wrong, treating them with fairness rather than dismissing their transmitters? What one step can you take this week to practice that intellectual humility?
?
If you ever discover that something you believed about your own family or history turns out to be uncertain, would your faith in Allah's guidance waver? What does this passage teach you about separating essential truth from secondary details?

The Lineage of Ibrahim: Trusting Sound Narrations
Ibn Asakir provides the lineage of Ibrahim from Ibn Ishaq and declares it sufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lineage of Ibrahim as transmitted by Ibn Ishaq and why Ibn Asakir considers it sufficient.

Core Idea
A single sound narration from a reliable scholar can settle a matter.

Ibn Asakir presents a complete chain of transmission reaching Ibn Ishaq, who provides the lineage of Ibrahim from Adam. He then states that this narration suffices, demonstrating the authority of trusted scholars in historical matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the lineage and the chain.
2
Examine the chain of transmission
Trace the chain back to Ibn Ishaq and note the multiple narrators involved.
The chain includes several trustworthy transmitters.
3
Analyze Ibn Asakir's conclusion
Consider why Ibn Asakir says that Ibn Ishaq's statement suffices. What does this reveal about scholarly authority?
This connects to the learning objective.

?

The Chain for al-Ash'ari's Conversion Story
Ibn Asakir begins explaining the reason Abu al-Hasan renounced his earlier beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the isnad that Ibn Asakir uses to introduce the reason for Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's return from his earlier beliefs.

Core Idea
Before the story, the chain—showing how the account was reliably transmitted.

This lesson opens the section on why Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari abandoned his previous theological position. Ibn Asakir, meticulous as always, first provides his chain of transmission to guarantee the account's authenticity. The passage here only presents the beginning of that chain; the narrative itself will follow in subsequent pages. Understanding this isnad is key to appreciating the trustworthiness of the story.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice that the passage ends abruptly with 'I heard' – it is incomplete, setting up a continuation.
This is a common feature in manuscript-based texts where page breaks occur mid-sentence.
2
Identify the narrators
Trace the chain: Ibn Asakir heard from Abu al-Muzaffar al-Bistami, who heard from his maternal grandfather Abu al-Fadl al-Sahlaki. Note the honorifics like 'al-shaykh al-zahid' (the ascetic scholar).
3
Ask: why is this chain important?
In Islamic historiography, a strong isnad lends credibility to the account. By naming these figures, Ibn Asakir assures the reader that the story comes from a reliable source.
This connects to the learning objective about the role of isnads.
4
Prepare for the coming narrative
This isnad is a prelude. In the next lesson, you will learn the actual reason for al-Ash'ari's return. For now, focus on the chain itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما سبب رجوع ابي الحسن عما كان عليه وتبريه مما كان يدعو اليه فأخبرني الشيخ أبو المظفر أحمد بن أبي العباس الحسن بن محمد البسطامي الشعيرى ببسطام، قال أنا جدى لأمى الشيخ الزاهد أبو الفضل محمد بن على بن أحمد بن الحسين بن سهل السهلكي البسطامي قال سمعت
As for the reason for Abu al-Hasan's return from what he had been upon and his disavowal of what he used to call to, Sheikh Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn Abi al-Abbas al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Bistami al-Sha'iri in Bistam informed me, he said: my maternal grandfather, the ascetic Sheikh Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Ahmad ibn al-Husayn ibn Sahl al-Sahlaki al-Bistami, said: I heard
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage begins a new section explaining why Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari abandoned his previous theological position (likely Mu'tazilism) and publicly repudiated it. Ibn Asakir provides his chain of transmission: he received the account from Sheikh Abu al-Muzaffar al-Bistami, who heard it from his grandfather, the ascetic Abu al-Fadl al-Sahlaki al-Bistami. The narrative stops mid-sentence at 'I heard', indicating the text continues on a later page. This serves as a preface to the historical account of al-Ash'ari's conversion.

Biographical Note on Ahmad Zaid
Ibn Asakir records Ahmad Zaid's governorship and conquests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a brief biographical note about Ahmad Zaid, his governorship under Umar and Uthman, and the significance of this record in historical chains.

Core Idea
Ahmad Zaid: a governor with military conquests under two caliphs.

In this short biographical excerpt, Ibn Asakir provides a snapshot of Ahmad Zaid's public roles during the early Islamic caliphate. The note mentions his governorships in Basra and Kufa, as well as his military achievements. Such biographical records form the backbone of historical and hadith transmission chains.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the words settle before analyzing details.
The passage is short, so pay attention to each phrase.
2
Identify key biographical details
List the positions Ahmad Zaid held (governor of Basra for Umar and Uthman, governor of Kufa) and his achievement (many conquests).
3
Examine the final phrase
Look at the ambiguous phrase 'وولد حضرة المسجد الجامع'. Consider its possible meanings: does it refer to a child attending the mosque, or could it be a name?
The OCR suggests this phrase may need manuscript verification.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Think about why Ibn Asakir includes such details. How does this biographical note help authenticate historical or hadith chains?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحمد زيد ولى البصرة لعمر وعثمان رضى الله عنهما وله بها فتوح كثيرة وولى الكوفة وله بها دار وولد حضرة المسجد الجامع
Ahmad Zaid was governor of Basra for Umar and Uthman, may Allah be pleased with them, and he had many conquests there, and he was governor of Kufa and had a house there, and a child attended the grand mosque.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a short biographical report about a figure named Ahmad Zaid. It states that he served as governor of Basra during the caliphates of Umar and Uthman, achieving many conquests. He also governed Kufa, where he owned a house. The final phrase 'وولد حضرة المسجد الجامع' is unclear but may indicate that his child attended the grand mosque. The purpose is to provide historical background on a person mentioned in the chain.

Key Terms
أحمد زيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'وولد حضرة المسجد الجامع' refers to a place called 'Hadrat al-Masjid al-Jami'
Correction: It likely means 'and a child attended the grand mosque' or could be a name, but it is not a place name.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which positions did Ahmad Zaid hold?
A) Governor of both Basra and Kufa
B) Governor of Basra only
C) Governor of Kufa only
D) He held no governorship

Key Takeaway
Ahmad Zaid served as governor under Umar and Uthman, leading conquests, and his biographical record is part of Ibn Asakir's historical compilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ahmad Zaid used his position as governor to achieve many conquests for Islam. You too have authority—over your family, your colleagues, or even just yourself. Are you using that authority to advance the deen, or to serve your own desires? What is one concrete step you can take today to change that?
?
He had a house and a child who attended the grand mosque. Does your home encourage your children (or those under your care) to attend the mosque and learn the deen? Or is it a place of distraction? What can you do this week to make your household more attached to the masjid?
?
Ahmad Zaid is remembered centuries later for his service under the caliphs. When you stand before Allah, what will your legacy be? Are you conquering your own nafs and following the Sunnah, or are you being conquered by worldly desires? Make a sincere dua now for steadfastness.
